RSVP Docs Pricing Sign In Get Demo Get Started ← Back to Blog Home Contents Sentry vs OpenTelemetry is the wrong question Direct OTLP vs Collector forwarding A demo architecture The frontend uses the Sentry SDK The backend keeps OpenTelemetry The checkout flow adds manual OTel spans and logs The Collector forwards OTLP to Sentry What each layer is responsible for What you can see in Sentry A decision tree for your own app Start with the smallest change that preserves your trace ShareYou don't need to pick one: how Sentry and OpenTelemetry work together Lazar Nikolov - May 27, 2026 · 9 min read TL;DR — If your backend already uses OpenTelemetry, you can send traces and logs to Sentry by changing a few environment variables. No SDK swap, no instrumentation rewrite. Point your OTLP exporter at Sentry's endpoint, add the Sentry SDK on the frontend for browser context, and you get one connected trace from click to backend span. You already instrumented the backend with OpenTelemetry. Your services emit spans. Your teams know the OTel APIs. Maybe you already run a Collector. So when you start evaluating Sentry, the obvious question is: Do you need to replace your OpenTelemetry setup with the Sentry SDK? No. The practical answer is usually: keep OpenTelemetry where it already works, add the Sentry SDK where it gives you more application context, and send OpenTelemetry Protocol (OTLP) events to Sentry. For a web app, that often means using the Sentry SDK on the frontend for browser tracing, errors, logs, Session Replay, and source maps, while keeping OpenTelemetry on the backend for existing service instrumentation. One scope note: OTLP can carry traces, logs, and metrics. At this moment, Sentry's OTLP ingest supports logs and traces, not metrics. We're considering adding support for them in the future. The important part is separating two decisions that often get lumped together: How traces stay connected across frontend and backend. How backend OTLP events are exported to Sentry. Once you separate those, the architecture gets a lot easier to reason about. Sentry vs OpenTelemetry is the wrong question The first decision is trace linking. If a user clicks a button in your React app and that click triggers a backend request, the frontend and backend need to agree on the same distributed trace context. In this example, the Sentry frontend SDK sends W3C traceparent headers (configurable through the propagateTraceparent option), and the OpenTelemetry backend continues the trace. That linking is handled by the frontend SDK configuration: Click to Copy The second decision is export. After your backend creates telemetry, where do those OTLP events go? There are two common options: Send OTLP events directly from the backend to Sentry's OTLP endpoint. Send OTLP events to an OpenTelemetry Collector, then have the Collector forward them to Sentry's OTLP endpoint. That trace-continuation step is what lets a Sentry-instrumented browser action become the parent of backend OpenTelemetry work, regardless of which OTLP export option you choose. If you want the reference docs for these pieces, start with linking Sentry SDKs with OpenTelemetry SDKs, sending OpenTelemetry traces directly to Sentry, sending OpenTelemetry logs directly to Sentry, and forwarding OpenTelemetry data to Sentry. Direct OTLP vs Collector forwarding Direct OTLP and Collector forwarding both end at Sentry's OTLP endpoint. The difference is whether your service talks to Sentry itself or talks to a Collector first. Direct OTLP is the simplest path for a single backend project: Click to Copy Collector forwarding is the better fit when your observability setup is already more than one app talking to one destination. It gives you one place to receive telemetry from many services, batch it, process it, and send it to one or more backends. That last part matters when you are evaluating Sentry. You can keep routing telemetry to an existing vendor while also forwarding a copy to Sentry, then compare the debugging experience without rewriting backend instrumentation. There is one important Sentry-specific detail for larger setups: a generic OTLP HTTP exporter points at one Sentry project endpoint with one project key. If you send every service through that one exporter, every service lands in the same Sentry project. For multi-project routing, use the Sentry exporter. It can route OTLP events to projects based on a resource attribute like service.name , and it can auto-create missing projects when configured with the right Sentry API permissions. A demo architecture Let's check out a demo project that uses the Collector forwarding path: Click to Copy The frontend lives in frontend/ . It is a React + Vite app using @sentry/react . The backend lives in backend/ . It is a FastAPI service using the OpenTelemetry SDK, FastAPI instrumentation, SQLAlchemy instrumentation, manual spans, and standard logging in checkout logic. The Collector lives in collector/ and forwards those OTLP events to Sentry. The point of the demo is not that every layer uses the same SDK. The point is that every layer participates in the same trace, with backend logs attached to that debugging context. The frontend uses the Sentry SDK The Sentry setup is in frontend/src/instrument.ts . It enables browser tracing, Session Replay, logs, and trace propagation: Click to Copy The frontend checkout flow is in frontend/src/hooks/useCheckoutLab.ts . It creates Sentry spans for user-facing work, logs useful state changes, and captures unexpected errors: Click to Copy The actual request is a normal fetch call: Click to Copy There is no manual trace header code in that request. The Sentry browser tracing integration handles the propagation as long as the destination matches tracePropagationTargets . The backend keeps OpenTelemetry The backend does not install or initialize the Sentry SDK. Its OpenTelemetry setup is in backend/app/core/observability.py . It creates an OpenTelemetry TracerProvider , attaches service resource attributes, exports spans over OTLP HTTP, and registers W3C trace-context propagation: Click to Copy It also configures OTLP log export. The backend creates a LoggerProvider , attaches an OTLPLogExporter , and adds an OpenTelemetry LoggingHandler to the app logger: Click to Copy The default backend endpoints are local: Click to Copy That means the backend exports OTLP traces and logs to the Collector, not directly to Sentry. The FastAPI app also allows the browser trace headers through CORS: Click to Copy This is easy to miss. If your browser is making cross-origin requests and CORS blocks the propagation headers, your frontend and backend traces can split apart even if both sides are instrumented correctly. The checkout flow adds manual OTel spans and logs The backend service code in backend/app/services/checkout.py models a checkout workflow. It creates manual OpenTelemetry spans for business operations: Click to Copy The checkout path includes spans for: checkout.validate_cart checkout.reserve_inventory checkout.calculate_tax checkout.payment checkout.write_order checkout.send_confirmation It also emits backend logs for the same business steps, such as checkout.started , checkout.cart_validated , checkout.inventory_reserved , checkout.payment_approved , checkout.order_written , and checkout.completed . Those logs go through Python's standard logging API, then the OpenTelemetry LoggingHandler exports them through OTLP. This is the part OTel-first teams care about most. Those spans and logs stay in the OpenTelemetry pipeline. You do not need to rewrite them with Sentry.startSpan() or Sentry logging APIs just to view the trace and related logs in Sentry. The Collector forwards OTLP to Sentry The Collector config is in collector/otel-collector.yaml . It receives OTLP over gRPC and HTTP: Click to Copy It batches the data: Click to Copy Then it forwards to Sentry with the OTLP HTTP exporter: Click to Copy Reminder: this demo uses generic otlphttp for a single Sentry project. For multi-project routing or automatic project creation, swap it for the sentry exporter. The configured pipelines send to both the debug exporter and Sentry: Click to Copy That is the Collector forwarding pattern: the backend sends OTLP events to one local endpoint, and the Collector decides where that telemetry goes next. What each layer is responsible for The cleanest way to understand this architecture is by ownership. The frontend Sentry SDK owns browser-specific debugging context: Browser spans and frontend transactions Frontend errors and React error boundaries Session Replay Frontend logs Source maps through the Sentry Vite plugin Trace propagation to the backend The backend OpenTelemetry SDK owns backend instrumentation: FastAPI request spans SQLAlchemy spans HTTPX spans if the backend calls other services Manual checkout spans Backend logs exported with OpenTelemetry Resource attributes such as service.name and deployment.environment OTLP export to the Collector The Collector owns routing and processing: Receiving OTLP on 4317 and 4318 Batching telemetry Printing debug output locally Forwarding to Sentry's OTLP endpoint Providing the place to add sampling, transforms, filters, or multi-vendor routing later This is why Sentry and OpenTelemetry are not competing choices here. They are doing different jobs in the same observability pipeline. What you can see in Sentry In the demo, I ran the checkout scenarios from the same frontend session. In Sentry, they show up as one connected trace that starts in React and continues through the FastAPI backend. You can see the normal checkout, slow payment, inventory miss, payment declined, and backend crash work in the same distributed trace instead of jumping between separate tools. The backend logs are associated with that trace too. Logs like checkout.started , checkout.inventory_reserved , checkout.payment_slow_path , and checkout.completed come from Python's standard logging API, get exported through OTLP, and land in Sentry attached to the same debugging context as the spans. That is the useful part of the setup: the frontend SDK gives you browser context, the backend keeps its OpenTelemetry spans and logs, and Sentry gives you one place to inspect the full request path. A decision tree for your own app Use direct OTLP to Sentry when: You have one backend service or one project. You want the fewest moving parts. You do not need central processing or multi-destination routing yet. Use Collector forwarding when: You already run an OpenTelemetry Collector. You have multiple backend services. You need services to land in separate Sentry projects. You need sampling, filtering, transforms, or batching outside the app process. You want to send telemetry to Sentry and another vendor while evaluating Sentry. Add the Sentry backend SDK later when: You want backend errors and exceptions captured by the Sentry SDK and linked to the trace, so you can jump from an exception to the full request path and inspect the related logs, metrics, profiles, and spans. You want profiling. You want Sentry's Application Metrics. You want Sentry features that are not represented by your current OpenTelemetry data. That last step is optional, not a prerequisite. You can start with Sentry on the frontend and OpenTelemetry on the backend, then decide later whether adding the backend Sentry SDK is worth it for your services. Start with the smallest change that preserves your trace If your backend already uses OpenTelemetry, do not start by rewriting instrumentation. Start by deciding where OTLP events should go. For a single backend, direct OTLP to Sentry is usually enough. For multiple services, vendor evaluation, or anything that needs routing and processing, put a Collector in the middle. Then link your frontend Sentry SDK to your backend OTel SDK with W3C traceparent propagation. That gives you the useful part first: one trace that starts where the user action starts and continues through the backend code you already instrumented. You do not need to pick Sentry or OpenTelemetry. Use both where they fit. FAQs Do I need to remove OpenTelemetry to use Sentry? ▾ No. If your backend is already instrumented with OpenTelemetry, keep that instrumentation and send its OTLP events to Sentry. Add the Sentry SDK where it gives you extra context, such as frontend errors, Session Replay, browser tracing, logs, and source maps. When should I use direct OTLP instead of a Collector? ▾ Use direct OTLP when you have one backend service or project and want the simplest possible setup. Use a Collector when you have multiple services, need processing or tail-sampling, or want to route telemetry to multiple backends (useful when evaluating Sentry). What do I lose if my backend only uses OpenTelemetry and not the Sentry SDK? ▾ You can still send backend OTLP events to Sentry. Backend-only Sentry SDK features such as profiling and SDK-specific context require adding the Sentry backend SDK or a Sentry OpenTelemetry integration later. Does Sentry ingest OTLP metrics? ▾ Not yet. At this moment, Sentry's OTLP ingest supports logs and traces, not metrics. We're working on supporting metrics as well. If you need metrics now, keep your existing metrics pipeline or use Sentry's Application Metrics through the Sentry SDK where supported. Dismiss alert open-telemetry / opentelemetry-collector-contrib Public Notifications You must be signed in to change notification settings Fork 3.6k Star 4.7k Code Issues 707 Pull requests 274 Discussions Actions Projects Security and quality 3 Insights Additional navigation options Code Issues Pull requests Discussions Actions Projects Security and quality Insights Files Expand file tree main Breadcrumbs opentelemetry-collector-contrib / processor / redactionprocessor / README.md Copy path Blame More file actions Blame More file actions Latest commit hawkaii [processor/redaction] document audit trail attributes in README (#46706) Open commit details success 3 months ago 60e7d87 · 3 months ago History History Open commit details History 343 lines (278 loc) · 16.6 KB · main Breadcrumbs opentelemetry-collector-contrib / processor / redactionprocessor / README.md Top File metadata and controls Preview Code Blame 343 lines (278 loc) · 16.6 KB · Raw Copy raw file Download raw file Outline Edit and raw actions Redaction Processor This processor deletes span, log, and metric datapoint attributes that don't match a list of allowed attributes. It also masks attribute values that match a blocked value list. Attributes that aren't on the allowed list are removed before any value checks are done. Use Cases Typical use-cases: Prevent sensitive fields from accidentally leaking into traces Ensure compliance with legal, privacy, or security requirements For example: EU General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) prohibits the transfer of any personal data like birthdates, addresses, or ip addresses across borders without explicit consent from the data subject. Popular trace aggregation services are located in US, not in EU. You can use the redaction processor to scrub personal data from your data. PRC legislation prohibits the transfer of geographic coordinates outside of the PRC. Popular trace aggregation services are located in US, not in the PRC. You can use the redaction processor to scrub geographic coordinates from your data. Payment Card Industry (PCI) Data Security Standards prohibit logging certain things or storing them unencrypted. You can use the redaction processor to scrub them from your traces. The above is written by an engineer, not a lawyer. The redaction processor is intended as one line of defence rather than the only compliance measure in place. Processor Configuration Please refer to config.go for the config spec. Examples: Refer to config.yaml for how to fit the configuration into an OpenTelemetry Collector pipeline definition. Ignored attributes are processed first so they're always allowed and never blocked. This field should only be used where you know the data is always safe to send to the telemetry system. You can use either ignored_keys for exact key matches or ignored_key_patterns for regex-based pattern matching. Only span/log/datapoint attributes included on the list of allowed keys list are retained. If allowed_keys is empty, then no attributes are allowed. All attributes are removed in that case. To keep all span attributes, you should explicitly set allow_all_keys to true. blocked_values and allowed_values applies to the values of the allowed keys. If the value of an allowed key matches the regular expression for an allowed value, the matching part of the value is not masked even if it matches the regular expression for a blocked value. If the value matches the regular expression for a blocked value only, the matching part of the value is masked with a fixed length of asterisks. Precedence between allowed_values and blocked_values When both allowed_values and blocked_values are configured, allowed_values takes precedence. This means that if a value matches an entry in allowed_values , it will not be masked even if it also matches blocked_values . This behavior is intentional and allows operators to explicitly whitelist known-safe values while still blocking broader patterns. Example blocked_key_patterns applies to the values of the keys matching one of the patterns. The value is then masked according to the configuration. hash_function defines the function for hashing values of matched keys or matches in values instead of masking them with a fixed string. By default, no hash function is used and masking with a fixed string is performed. The supported hash functions are md5 , sha1 , sha3 (SHA-256), hmac-sha256 , and hmac-sha512 . HMAC Hash Functions For enhanced security, especially when dealing with low-entropy data like IP addresses, HMAC (Hash-based Message Authentication Code) hash functions are recommended over simple hash functions like MD5, SHA1, or SHA3. Configuration Example Audit Trail When summary is set to debug or info , the processor appends diagnostic attributes to each span, log record, or metric datapoint describing the actions it took. Setting summary: silent suppresses all audit attributes. Attribute-level audit (spans, logs, metric datapoints) These attributes are added to the record's attribute map: Log body audit For log records whose body is a map, the processor additionally appends audit attributes into the body map itself: Example Given this configuration: A span arriving with these attributes: Would be emitted with: Note that credit_card was removed (not masked) because it was not in allowed_keys — its value never reached the blocked_values check. If credit_card had been in allowed_keys , its value 4111111111111111 would have matched the Visa pattern and redaction.masked.keys would show "credit_card" instead. Attributes with a zero count (e.g. redaction.masked.count , redaction.ignored.count ) are not emitted — the processor only writes audit attributes when at least one relevant action occurred. URL Sanitization The url_sanitizer configuration enables sanitization of URLs in specified attributes by removing potentially sensitive information like UUIDs, timestamps, and other non-essential path segments. This is particularly useful for reducing cardinality in telemetry data while preserving the essential parts of URLs for troubleshooting. Span Name Sanitization By default, when URL sanitization is enabled, span names for client and server span types that contain "/" characters are automatically sanitized. This helps reduce cardinality issues caused by high-variability URL paths in span names while preserving essential routing information. You can control this behavior using the sanitize_span_name option: true (default): Span names will be sanitized along with attributes false : Only attributes are sanitized, span names remain unchanged This option is available independently for both URL and database sanitization, allowing fine-grained control over which span names should be redacted. For example, if notes is on the list of allowed keys, then the notes attribute is retained. However, if there is a value such as a credit card number in the notes field that matched a regular expression on the list of blocked values, then that value is masked. Database Query Sanitization The redaction processor now supports sanitizing database queries and commands to remove sensitive information. This feature supports multiple database systems: SQL databases Redis Memcached MongoDB OpenSearch Elasticsearch Example configuration with database sanitization: The database sanitizer will: Remove sensitive data like literal values from SQL queries Redact command arguments from Redis/Memcached commands Sanitize MongoDB queries and JSON payloads Process only specified attributes if provided Preserve query structure while removing sensitive data Sanitize span names containing database queries (can be controlled with sanitize_span_name ) By default, database query sanitization also applies to span names for client span types. You can disable this behavior by setting sanitize_span_name: false in the db_sanitizer configuration, which allows you to keep original database query span names while still sanitizing the query values in attributes. This provides an additional layer of protection when collecting telemetry that includes database operations. Trace and metric behaviour: Database sanitization for spans and metric attributes only runs when the telemetry includes a db.system.name or db.system attribute and the span kind is CLIENT or SERVER . This prevents non-database spans from being rewritten. Logs automatically enable a sequential fallback internally, so database attributes without db.system can still be sanitized when they appear in log records. Footer © 2026 GitHub, Inc. [-] TroublesShooting Node.js Tracing Issues [-] Welcome [-] Erlang/Elixir, JS, and Ruby 1.0 [-] 2021 [-] Auto-instrumentation in Kubernetes [-] 2021 Governance Committee [-] C++ 1.0 [-] Trace-Based Testing with Malabi [-] 2021 GC election [-] Collector GA release [-] Swift 1.0 Beta [-] Python 1.0 [-] Women's day 2021 [-] 2019 [-] Governance Committee Explained View Markdown View page source Edit this page Create child page Create documentation issue On this page 2025: Year of AI agents What is an AI agent Observability and beyond Current state of AI agent observability Understanding AI agent application vs. AI agent framework Establishing a standardized semantic convention Instrumentation approaches Option 1: Baked-in instrumentation Option 2: Instrumentation via OpenTelemetry Future of AI agent observability Role of OpenTelemetry's GenAI SIG Blog 2025 AI Agent Observability AI Agent Observability - Evolving Standards and Best Practices By Guangya Liu (IBM), Sujay Solomon (Google) | Thursday, March 06, 2025 Blog posts are not updated after publication. This post is more than a year old, so its content may be outdated, and some links may be invalid. Cross-verify any information before relying on it. 2025: Year of AI agents AI Agents are becoming the next big leap in artificial intelligence in 2025. From autonomous workflows to intelligent decision making, AI Agents will power numerous applications across industries. However, with this evolution comes the critical need for AI agent observability, especially when scaling these agents to meet enterprise needs. Without proper monitoring, tracing, and logging mechanisms, diagnosing issues, improving efficiency, and ensuring reliability in AI agent-driven applications will be challenging. What is an AI agent An AI agent is an application that uses a combination of LLM capabilities, tools to connect to the external world, and high-level reasoning to achieve a desired end goal or state; Alternatively, agents can also be treated as systems where LLMs dynamically direct their own processes and tool usage, maintaining control over how they accomplish tasks. Image credit: Google AI Agent Whitepaper. For more information about AI agents, see: Google: What is an AI agent? IBM: What are AI agents? MicroSoft: AI agents — what they are, and how they'll change the way we work AWS: What are AI Agents? Anthropic: Building effective agents Observability and beyond Typically, telemetry from applications is used to monitor and troubleshoot them. In the case of an AI agent, given its non-deterministic nature, telemetry is also used as a feedback loop to continuously learn from and improve the quality of the agent by using it as input for evaluation tools. Given that observability and evaluation tools for GenAI come from various vendors, it is important to establish standards around the shape of the telemetry generated by agent apps to avoid lock-in caused by vendor or framework specific formats. Current state of AI agent observability As AI agent ecosystems continue to mature, the need for standardized and robust observability has become more apparent. While some frameworks offer built-in instrumentation, others rely on integration with observability tools. This fragmented landscape underscores the importance of the GenAI observability project and OpenTelemetry's emerging semantic conventions, which aim to unify how telemetry data is collected and reported. Understanding AI agent application vs. AI agent framework It is crucial to distinguish between AI agent application and AI agent frameworks: AI agent application refer to individual AI-driven entities that perform specific tasks autonomously. AI agent framework provide the necessary infrastructure to develop, manage, and deploy AI agents often in a more streamlined way than building an agent from scratch. Examples include the following: IBM Bee AI, IBM wxFlow, CrewAI, AutoGen, Semantic Kernel, LangGraph, PydanticAI and more. Establishing a standardized semantic convention Today, the GenAI observability project within OpenTelemetry is actively working on defining semantic conventions to standardize AI agent observability. This effort is primarily driven by: Agent application semantic convention – A draft AI agent application semantic convention has already been established and finalized as part of the discussions in the OpenTelemetry semantic conventions repository. The initial AI agent semantic convention is based on Google's AI agent white paper, providing a foundational framework for defining observability standards. Moving forward, we will continue to refine and enhance this initial convention to make it more robust and comprehensive. Agent framework semantic convention – Now, the focus has shifted towards defining a common semantic convention for all AI agent frameworks. This effort is being discussed in this OpenTelemetry issue and aims to establish a standardized approach for frameworks such as IBM Bee Stack, IBM wxFlow, CrewAI, AutoGen, LangGraph, and others. Additionally, different AI Agent frameworks will be able to define their own Framework Vendor Specific Semantic Convention while adhering to the common standard. By establishing these conventions, we ensure that AI agent frameworks can report standardized metrics, traces, and logs, making it easier to integrate observability solutions and compare performance across different frameworks. Note: Experimental conventions already exist in OpenTelemetry for models at GenAI semantic convention. Instrumentation approaches In order to make a system observable, it must be instrumented: That is, code from the system's components must emit traces, metrics, and logs. Different AI agent frameworks have varying approaches to implementing observability, mainly categorized into two options: Option 1: Baked-in instrumentation The first option is to implement built-in instrumentation that emits telemetry using OpenTelemetry semantic conventions. This means observability is a native feature, allowing users to seamlessly track agent performance, task execution, and resource utilization. Some AI agent frameworks, such as CrewAI, follow this pattern. As a developer of an agent framework, here are some pros and cons of this baked-in instrumentation: Pros You can take on the maintenance overhead of keeping the instrumentation for telemetry up-to-date. Simplifies adoption for users unfamiliar with OpenTelemetry configuration. Keep new features secret while providing instrumentation for them on the day of release. Cons Adds bloat to the framework for users who do not need observability features. Risk of version lock-in if the framework's OpenTelemetry dependencies lag behind upstream updates. Less flexibility for advanced users who prefer custom instrumentation. You may not get feedback/review from OTel contributors familiar with current semantic conventions. Your instrumentation may lag with respect to best practices/conventions (not just the version of the OTel library dependencies). Some best practices to follow if you consider this approach: Provide a configuration setting that lets users easily enable or disable telemetry collection from your framework's built-in instrumentation. Plan ahead of users wanting to use other external instrumentation packages and avoid collision. Consider listing your agent framework in the OpenTelemetry registry if you choose this path. As a developer of an agent application, you may want to choose an agent framework with baked-in instrumentation if… Minimal dependencies on external packages in your agent app code. Out-of-the-box observability without manual setup. Option 2: Instrumentation via OpenTelemetry This option is to publish OpenTelemetry instrumentation libraries to some GitHub repositories. These instrumentation libraries can be imported into agents and configured to emit telemetry per OpenTelemetry semantic conventions. For publishing instrumentation with OpenTelemetry, there are two options: Option 1: External instrumentation in your own repository/package, like Traceloop OpenTelemetry Instrumentation, Langtrace OpenTelemetry Instrumentation etc. Option 2: External instrumentation in OpenTelemetry owned repository, like instrumentation-genai etc. Both options work well, but the long term goal is to host the code in OpenTelemetry owned repositories, like Traceloop is trying to donate the instrumentation code to OpenTelemetry now. As a developer of an agent framework, here are some pros and cons of instrumentation with OpenTelemetry: Pros Decouples observability from the core framework, reducing bloat. Leverages OpenTelemetry's community-driven maintenance for instrumentation updates. Allows users to mix and match contrib libraries for their specific needs (e.g., cloud providers, LLM vendors). More likely to leverage best practices around semantic conventions and zero-code instrumentation Cons Risk of fragmentation if users rely on incompatible or outdated contrib packages for both install time and runtime. Development velocity slows down when there are too many PRs in the OpenTelemetry review queue. Best practices for this approach: Ensure compatibility with popular OpenTelemetry contrib libraries (e.g., LLM vendors, vector DBs). Provide clear documentation on recommended contrib packages and configuration examples. Avoid reinventing the wheel; align with existing OpenTelemetry standards. As a developer of an agent application, you may want to choose an agent framework with baked-in instrumentation if… You need fine-grained control over telemetry sources and destinations. Your use case requires integrating observability with niche or custom tools. NOTE: Regardless of the approach taken, it is essential that all AI agent frameworks adopt the AI agent framework semantic convention to ensure interoperability and consistency in observability data. Future of AI agent observability Looking ahead, AI agent observability will continue to evolve with: More robust semantic conventions to cover edge cases and emerging AI agent frameworks. A unified AI agent framework semantic convention to ensure interoperability across different frameworks while allowing flexibility for vendor-specific extensions. Continuous improvements to the AI agent semantic convention to refine the initial standard and address new challenges as AI agents evolve. Improved tooling for monitoring, debugging, and optimizing AI agents. Tighter integration with AI model observability to provide end-to-end visibility into AI powered applications. Role of OpenTelemetry's GenAI SIG The GenAI Special Interest Group (SIG) in OpenTelemetry is actively defining GenAI semantic conventions that cover key areas such as: LLM or model semantic conventions VectorDB semantic conventions AI agent semantic conventions (a critical component within the broader GenAI semantic convention) In addition to conventions, the SIG has also expanded its scope to provide instrumentation coverage for agents and models in Python and other languages. As AI Agents become increasingly sophisticated, observability will play a fundamental role in ensuring their reliability, efficiency, and trustworthiness. Establishing a standardized approach to AI Agent observability requires collaboration, and we invite contributions from the broader AI community. We look forward to partnering with different AI agent framework communities to establish best practices and refine these standards together. Your insights and contributions will help shape the future of AI observability, fostering a more transparent and effective AI ecosystem. Don't miss this opportunity to help shape the future of industry standards for GenAI Observability! Join us on the CNCF Slack #otel-genai-instrumentation channel, or by attending a GenAI SIG meeting. ← Previous Next → Last modified February 24, 2026: chore(blog/2025-26): use full commit hash instead of main in GH repo URL (#9272) (f9d321fb) © 2019–present OpenTelemetry Authors | Docs CC BY 4.0 All Rights Reserved Ask AI
Source: Architectural Blueprint for Enterprise Observability: Standards, Optimization, and Contextual TrackingType: Pasted TextArchitectural Blueprint for Enterprise Observability: Standards, Optimization, and Contextual TrackingOpen Standard Ingestion and the Mitigation of Vendor Lock-InThe contemporary enterprise software ecosystem relies on distributed microservices and dynamic cloud-native architectures, making application telemetry an absolute prerequisite for system reliability.[1, 2, 3] Within this environment, establishing a standardized, open-source, and vendor-neutral collection layer is critical to mitigating the structural risk of vendor lock-in.[1, 4] Historically, application performance monitoring (APM) providers bundled telemetry collection with backend analytical engines, enforcing proprietary SDKs and custom agents.[1, 4] When contract rates escalate or vendor capabilities stagnate, organizations utilizing proprietary telemetry pipelines face severe switching costs.[4] For example, migrating code-based proprietary instrumentation across a standard enterprise fleet of 47 microservices can consume up to 12 engineer-months of effort to manually refactor codebase dependencies.[4] This friction severely limits commercial leverage during contract renewals.[4]OpenTelemetry, which arose from the structural merger of the OpenTracing and OpenCensus projects, resolves this dependency trap by establishing a community-driven, open standard for collecting, processing, and exporting traces, metrics, and logs.[1, 5] Today, approximately 79% of organizations use or are actively considering OpenTelemetry to establish standardized, interoperable ingestion frameworks.[2] OpenTelemetry decouples telemetry generation from downstream storage, allowing engineering teams to change backend destinations via configuration edits at the collector tier, completing in days what previously required months of manual code rewrites.[1, 4]To preserve this flexibility, software architects must enforce a strict separation between provider initialization and the underlying instrumentation calls.[5] The OpenTelemetry client architecture facilitates this by separating the API from the SDK.[6] The application codebase imports only the lightweight OpenTelemetry API to generate spans, metrics, and logs.[5, 6] Because this API contains no vendor-specific code or SDK configurations, the core codebase remains decoupled from downstream providers.[5, 6] The SDK implementation, which manages the processing and exporting configurations, is initialized solely at the application's entry point.[5, 7] Under this architecture, transitioning to an alternative backend is achieved by editing environment variables or modifying the top-level provider initialization, leaving the core application logic untouched.[5]The choice of span processing mechanics during initialization also carries significant performance implications.[5, 8] OpenTelemetry provides two core options:SimpleSpanProcessor: Synchronously processes and exports each span immediately upon execution.[5, 8] While useful for local debugging, this model introduces severe thread blocking and network latency on high-throughput production paths.[7, 8]BatchSpanProcessor: Batches spans asynchronously and sends them in bulk to the collector tier.[7, 8] This is the recommended production pattern, as it significantly reduces connection overhead, prevents serialization bottlenecks within the application path, and optimizes overall transport throughput.[7, 8]This architectural standardization is especially critical in emerging paradigms, such as the deployment of GenAI frameworks and AI agents (including platforms like IBM Bee Stack, IBM wxFlow, CrewAI, AutoGen, and LangGraph).[9] Without industry-wide standards, these systems generate highly fragmented telemetry formats.[9] To prevent early-stage lock-in, the OpenTelemetry GenAI Special Interest Group (SIG) is actively defining standard semantic conventions for models, vector databases, and AI agents.[9]When implementing observability inside GenAI frameworks, teams must evaluate baked-in instrumentation against external OpenTelemetry contrib libraries.[9] While baked-in configurations simplify initial adoption for developers, they risk bloating the framework runtime and introducing version lock-in if the framework's internal OpenTelemetry dependencies fall behind upstream updates.[9] Conversely, utilizing external, modular contrib libraries (such as instrumentation-genai) allows teams to cleanly separate AI runtime concerns from telemetry dependencies, facilitating independent patching and scaling.[9]Downstream, the database engine and querying languages used for analysis present another significant lock-in risk.[10] Many commercial backends rely on proprietary domain-specific languages (DSLs).[10] These DSLs not only lock teams into a vendor's toolset but also increase training costs and limit deep analytical capabilities.[10] Choosing a backend that natively supports standard SQL empowers teams to integrate telemetry with external business intelligence, security, and data science platforms, facilitating exploratory analysis of "unknown unknowns".[10]The underlying database must also handle high-cardinality telemetry data (such as transactional user IDs, container identifiers, or individual trace IDs) without experiencing performance degradation.[10] Traditional row-oriented time-series databases (TSDBs) struggle with high-cardinality data, as each unique label combination triggers a combinatorial explosion of unique metric streams, resulting in indexing bottlenecks and memory exhaustion.[10] Columnar database architectures, such as ClickHouse, resolve this bottleneck by indexing and scanning data column by column, enabling sub-second analytical aggregations across petabyte-scale high-cardinality datasets.[10]Standardization of Semantic Conventions and Naming ArchitecturesAchieving consistent, actionable visibility across an enterprise requires uniform naming standards.[12] When independent development teams deploy isolated microservices, naming fragmentation (such as one team logging user_id while another logs customerId) creates chaos in the central logging and query pipelines.[12] OpenTelemetry resolves this by establishing Semantic Conventions.[12, 13] These conventions define standardized attribute names, metric labels, resource tags, and span properties to ensure uniform data structures across the entire organization.[12, 13]OpenTelemetry Semantic Conventions utilize a dot-separated namespace hierarchy following a logical pattern:\text{{namespace}}.\text{{object}}.\text{{attribute}}For example, HTTP request methods are recorded as http.request.method, and database types are logged as db.system.[12] Size and count attributes must explicitly define their units within the attribute name, such as messaging.message.payload_size_bytes or http.request.duration_ms.[12] This structure allows downstream observability platforms to index and organize telemetry into logical hierarchies, improving overall search and navigation performance.[14]Resource semantic conventions are uniquely critical, as they standardize metadata representing the underlying entity emitting the data, such as a microservice, serverless function, Kubernetes pod, or physical host.[15] These resource attributes establish service identity and placement:service.name: Defines the logical name of the application service.[15]service.version: Captures the release version or Git commit hash.[15]service.instance.id: Uniquely identifies a specific running container or host instance.[15]deployment.environment.name: Identifies the operational environment, such as production or staging.[15]Standardizing on these OTel keys ensures out-of-the-box compatibility with major commercial backends.[16] For example, backends like Datadog map standard OTel tags directly to their proprietary internal metadata schemas, ensuring consistent service tagging and platform integration.[16]For custom attributes that fall outside standard domain conventions, organizations must establish a rigorous custom naming architecture based on four core principles [15]:Unique Namespace Prefixes: Prevent naming collisions with standard OTel keys or third-party libraries by prefixing custom attributes with a reverse domain name (e.g., com.company.product.id) or a simplified system prefix (e.g., myapp.).[15]Logical and Consistent Structure: Custom keys must be lowercase and dot-separated, establishing a predictable, browsable hierarchy (e.g., app.shopping_cart.value).[15]Respect Standard Namespaces: Custom attributes must never be placed within protected standard namespaces, such as http., db., or messaging..[15]Enforce Cardinality Guardrails: Evaluate the cardinality of any attribute before appending it to metrics.[15]A well-defined metric in OpenTelemetry is built upon four pillars: its instrument type, its name, its unit, and its attributes.[15] Developers must match the physical metric behavior with the correct OTel instrument type [15]:Counter: A value that only ever increases, used for cumulative metrics like total HTTP request counts.[15]UpDownCounter: A value that can increase or decrease, used for transient values like active database connection pools.[15]Gauge: A snapshot measurement of a value at a specific point in time, such as current CPU or memory utilization.[15]Histogram: A statistical distribution of measurements across pre-defined buckets, used to compute averages, sums, and percentiles for values like request latency.[15]To maintain naming consistency across development teams, organizations should distribute shared utility libraries (via NuGet, npm, pip, or Go modules) that export standardized naming constants, enum structures, and helper methods.[14, 17] These shared packages eliminate "magic strings" in code, preventing errors where different teams compile inconsistent values, such as using pre-production versus pre-prod.[14, 17]These packages should also utilize inline code documentation tools (such as Javadoc, Godoc, or XMLdoc) to explain attribute requirements directly within developers' IDEs, improving adoption and data quality.[14, 17] At the collector tier, the OpenTelemetry Transformation Language (OTTL) can be used to reshape raw telemetry logs—for instance, using OTTL statements to set standard trace IDs from incoming attributes via set(trace_id,...) and deleting redundant metadata with delete_key(attributes,...) to ensure compliance and control storage costs before exporting.[18]Unified Log-Trace Correlation and Context PropagationBidirectional Signal MappingWhen a critical production failure occurs, engineering teams must immediately correlate the broad flow of a request with the fine-grained runtime logs of individual microservices.[19, 20] Without systematic correlation, debugging is slowed down by manual searching and copying of trace IDs across disparate indexes.[19, 20]Injecting OpenTelemetry trace IDs and span IDs directly into structured logs establishes a bidirectional link, enabling troubleshooting tools to jump instantly from an anomalous trace span to its corresponding logs, or vice-versa.[20, 21]Structured logs must include the trace_id, span_id, and trace_flags fields as top-level keys to allow backend indexing engines to map these correlations natively.[20]Context Propagation StandardsDistributed tracing relies on context propagation to pass trace metadata across physical process and network boundaries.[8, 22] When a service initiates an outbound call, context propagation serializes the active trace state into a standard format.[22, 23] The downstream service extracts and deserializes this context to continue the trace.[22, 23]The industry standard for context propagation is the W3C Trace Context specification.[22, 24] This standard defines two primary HTTP headers:traceparent Header: A single, structured string containing the essential trace properties, formatted as:\text{traceparent} = \text{version} - \text{trace_id} - \text{parent_span_id} - \text{trace_flags}In the example 00-4bf92f3577b34da6a3ce929d0e0e4736-00f067aa0ba902b7-01, 00 represents the W3C version, 4bf92f3577b34da6a3ce929d0e0e4736 is the 16-byte global trace ID, 00f067aa0ba902b7 is the 8-byte parent span ID, and 01 represents the bitwise flag indicating that the trace has been sampled.[22, 24, 25]tracestate Header: A comma-separated list of key-value pairs designed to carry vendor-specific routing and sampling metadata without mutating the primary traceparent context.[22, 24] It supports up to 32 entries with lowercase keys, allowing legacy and proprietary tracing systems to interoperate alongside standard OpenTelemetry deployments.[6, 22, 24]Complementing these tracing headers is the W3C Baggage specification.[8, 22] Baggage allows developers to propagate arbitrary key-value pairs (e.g., tenant_id, user_tier, or feature_flags) across the entire service topology.[8, 22] Because baggage travels in-band with every network request, it introduces transport overhead and must be used with caution.[8, 22] The W3C specification recommends limiting individual baggage entries to 180 characters, with a maximum cumulative baggage size of 8KB.[22]Inter-Service and Cross-Protocol Context PropagationWhile HTTP context propagation is easily handled by auto-instrumentation libraries, non-HTTP boundaries require custom architectural design.[23, 24]gRPC Transactions: gRPC relies on metadata, implemented on top of HTTP/2 headers.[26] OpenTelemetry propagates context over gRPC using either the standard traceparent header key or the binary-encoded grpc-trace-bin metadata key.[26]Message Queues (Kafka, RabbitMQ, SQS, Azure Service Bus): Because asynchronous messaging is decoupled, context cannot be propagated via active runtime threads.[8, 23, 25] Instead, the producing service must inject the traceparent and tracestate values directly into the message's metadata properties or headers.[8, 23, 25] Upon consuming the message, the worker extracts these headers, instantiates a new child span linked to the parent context, and processes the job.[8, 23]Fallback Mechanisms for Async Logging: In custom runtime setups, command-line interfaces, or background tasks where an active span context is entirely absent, applications should implement deterministic fallback identifiers.[27] For example, if no active span is detected, a stable trace_id can be derived by hashing a local session_id using SHA-256.[27] This ensures that even disconnected background operations maintain stable log correlation keys.[27]Client-Side Integration: Single-page applications (SPAs) should use OpenTelemetry JS to generate client-side correlation IDs and inject traceparent headers into API calls.[8] This ensures that user actions on the frontend are directly linked to downstream backend execution traces.[8]Language-Specific Instrumentation ArchitecturesTo implement log-trace correlation, development teams must configure their logging frameworks to extract and format OpenTelemetry context fields on every log write.[20, 21]In Python applications, the OpenTelemetry Python SDK provides a log handler wrapper that automatically injects trace context into log records.[20] This is typically enabled by invoking LoggingInstrumentor().instrument(set_logging_format=True) during initialization.[20] A structured JSON formatter then maps the injected attributes (e.g., otelTraceID and otelSpanID) directly into the log payload, ensuring every subsequent log call inside an active span automatically includes trace context.[20]Java applications utilizing SLF4J and Logback achieve trace-log correlation using Mapped Diagnostic Context (MDC) fields.[20, 21] The OpenTelemetry Java agent can be configured via otel-config.yaml to automatically inject trace identifiers into the MDC.[20] Developers then wrap their console logging appender inside an OpenTelemetryAppender block within logback.xml.[21] This ensures that fields like %X{trace_id} and %X{span_id} are formatted on every log line without requiring manual code modifications.[20, 21]In Node.js applications, teams using the Pino logging framework should implement a custom mixin function that runs on every log call.[20] This mixin calls the active OpenTelemetry API using trace.getSpan(context.active()).[20] If an active span is found, the mixin extracts traceId, spanId, and the trace flags, merging them directly into the structured JSON log output.[20]Go applications utilizing the standard log/slog package can enforce trace injection using a custom middleware wrapper.[20] Developers define a custom slog.Handler that intercept the Go execution context on every log write.[20] This handler extracts the active span using trace.SpanFromContext(ctx), retrieves the identifiers, and appends them as standard attributes to the structured log record before forwarding it to the downstream logging system.[20]Intelligent Sampling Strategies and Financial Lifecycle ManagementBalancing Cost and System VisibilityDeploying distributed tracing at scale can generate massive volumes of data, leading to high storage and network egress costs.[28, 29] Sampling is the primary mechanism utilized to control this volume, allowing organizations to collect a representative subset of traces that accurately reflects system behavior.[29, 30]Choosing the right sampling strategy requires balancing the performance impact on the monitored application against the granularity of downstream visibility.[29]Head-Based SamplingHead-based sampling makes the retention decision at the very beginning of a trace, typically at the root span.[28, 31, 32] The root service evaluates a probabilistic or rule-based sampler, determines whether to keep or drop the entire trace, and encodes this decision directly in the W3C traceparent header flags (using 01 to denote keep and 00 to denote drop).[28, 32] This flag is then propagated downstream, ensuring that all subsequent microservices follow the identical sampling decision.[28, 32]This upfront decision model minimizes computational and transport costs, as dropped spans are discarded immediately at the application source.[28, 29, 30] This prevents CPU serialization cycles and saves network egress bandwidth.[29, 30, 32] Under the hood, OpenTelemetry samplers return a sampling decision that controls the SDK's execution path: if a trace is dropped, the IsRecording flag is set to false, instructing the SDK to discard attributes, events, and links, while the Sampled flag is set to false to block exportation.[30]However, because head-based sampling occurs before any operations execute, it is completely blind to runtime errors, database failures, or latency anomalies.[28, 30] A 10% head-based sampler will discard 90% of all traces, including 90% of system errors and critical failures.[28]Tail-Based SamplingTail-based sampling inverts this model by delaying the retention decision until the complete distributed trace has fully executed across all microservices.[28, 29, 32] Under this model, application components are configured to sample 100% of spans locally, forwarding them to a centralized intermediate tier—typically an OpenTelemetry Collector cluster.[28, 31, 32] The collector buffers incoming spans, reconstructs the complete trace topology using the trace_id, and evaluates the trace against predefined tail-sampling rules once all spans have arrived.[28, 29]Tail-sampling policies are highly expressive, allowing teams to curate their storage toward anomalies.[28, 29] A standard production policy might enforce the following logic [28, 29]:Retain 100% of traces containing spans with errors (status code >= 500 or marked as ERROR).[28, 29]Retain 100% of traces where the total duration exceeds 2000ms.[28]Retain 100% of traces originating from high-value critical endpoints (e.g., /checkout or /payment).[28, 33]Retain a flat 5% baseline sample of standard, healthy transactions to maintain historical statistical baselines.[28]While tail-based sampling ensures unmatched visibility into system failures, its infrastructure requirements are intensive.[29, 32] The collector must maintain large memory buffers to hold traces in flight, introducing significant scaling challenges.[29, 32] If trace spans are routed across different collector instances rather than being pinned to a single collector node via routing hashes, the collector will fail to reconstruct the trace tree, resulting in incomplete, fragmented traces.[29, 31, 32] This is particularly challenging on platforms that do not natively support tail-sampling (such as Google Cloud Trace), requiring teams to deploy and manage their own intermediate collector infrastructure running the Tail Sampling Processor.[31]Layered Hybrid Sampling StrategyTo mitigate the infrastructure costs of pure tail-based sampling while avoiding the data loss of head-based sampling, mature engineering teams implement a layered, hybrid sampling strategy.[28] This architecture divides sampling into three distinct, cooperative tiers [28]:Layer 1: Head-Based Pre-Filter: Apply a high-volume, probabilistic head-based sampler at the application level (e.g., 20%).[28] This caps the raw volume of spans entering the collector tier, protecting the network and lowering collector memory requirements.[28]Layer 2: Tail-Based Priority Rules: Within the collector tier, process the remaining 20% sample through tail-sampling processors.[28] Keep 100% of errors or latency anomalies that survived the initial pre-filter, while down-sampling the standard healthy traces within that 20% group to a minimal 5% baseline.[28]Layer 3: Priority Routing for Critical Paths: Bypass the Layer 1 pre-filter entirely for critical endpoints.[28] Configure rules that enforce 100% head-based sampling for payment processors, authentication endpoints, or liveness paths where losing a single transaction trace is unacceptable.[28]Adaptive and Load-Based SamplingAdaptive sampling dynamically scales sampling rates based on real-time system performance and operational load.[8, 33] Rather than utilizing static percentages, the application or collector monitors system metrics (such as CPU, memory usage, or ingestion backpressure) and dynamically adjusts the sampling ratio.[8, 33] For example, under normal operating conditions, a service may collect a high volume of telemetry.[33] However, if the service encounters an unexpected traffic spike, the system reads a dynamic priority attribute and invokes a memory limiter, shedding low-priority telemetry (such as standard liveness probes) to preserve system stability and control egress budgets.[8, 33]Pipeline Optimization and Telemetry Overhead ControlTelemetry Ingestion Performance ImpactDeploying telemetry instrumentation introduces an unavoidable runtime penalty across several dimensions [33]:CPU Ingestion Overhead: Expended during span context creation, thread context propagation, attribute generation, and data serialization (e.g., converting object graphs to JSON or Protobuf).[33]Memory Footprint: Spans, logs, and metrics must be buffered in memory before being exported, which increases heap size and garbage collection (GC) frequency.[33, 34]Network Utilization: Transporting telemetry payloads consumes internal bandwidth and generates significant cloud egress costs.[32, 33, 34]Application Latency: Blocked threads or synchronous exporters can directly degrade application response times, particularly on high-throughput critical paths.[8, 33]OpenTelemetry Collector Pipeline ArchitectureTo manage this overhead, organizations must route local telemetry to an OpenTelemetry Collector.[1, 11] The collector operates as a highly optimized, modular proxy utilizing independent, parallel pipelines for traces, metrics, and logs.[11, 35]The collector's modular architecture consists of three core components [11, 35]:Receivers: Ingest telemetry in standard or third-party formats (e.g., OTLP/gRPC, Prometheus scraping, or raw host file logs).[11, 18, 35]Processors: Perform data manipulation, filtering, and safety operations sequentially within the pipeline.[35, 36] Critical processors include memory_limiter, batch, and transform.[11, 18, 35]Exporters: Format and dispatch processed telemetry to downstream storage backends or external SaaS providers.[11, 18, 35]These pipelines are configured in a centralized YAML file (/etc/otelcol/config.yaml), separating core processing capabilities from core agent dependencies.[11, 36] Developers can select between Core distributions (minimal, highly optimized processing blocks) and Contrib distributions, which package community-maintained receivers and exporters for third-party platforms like Kafka, MongoDB, or Redis.[11]Operational Optimization ChecklistBy applying structured configurations to the collector and SDK layers, architects can reduce performance overhead by 50% or more [33]:Tuning Batch Processing: Spans and logs should be buffered and exported in batches (typically 512 to 1024 spans) with appropriate timeouts (10 to 30 seconds) and parallel sender queues to prevent downstream blocking.[33, 37]Compression and Serialization Protocols: Exporters should utilize binary formats (Protobuf) over text-based formats (JSON).[2, 33] Utilizing gRPC-based OTLP exporters (otlptracegrpc) rather than HTTP/JSON (otlptracehttp) reduces network payload size by 40–50%.[33] Furthermore, enabling gzip or zstd compression within the exporter reduces overall network utilization by approximately 85%.[33, 37]Lazy Attribute Collection: Appending complex attributes (such as system diagnostics, environment dumps, or heavy SQL query parameters) to every span introduces severe CPU penalties.[33] Applications should initiate spans with minimal, lightweight attributes.[33] Expensive attributes should only be queried and appended conditionally once the head-based sampler has confirmed that the active trace is selected for downstream retention.[33]Selective Path Exclusions: Naive "instrument everything" strategies generate massive volumes of empty, low-value telemetry.[33] Engineers should configure instrumentation packages to explicitly exclude high-volume, low-value endpoints, such as Kubernetes liveness and readiness probes (/health) or Prometheus metrics scraping loops (/metrics).[33]Collector Stability and Memory LimitingThe collector must be actively protected from out-of-memory (OOM) failures caused by sudden traffic spikes or downstream backend outages.[33, 38] The primary safeguard is the memory_limiter processor, which must be configured as the very first processor in every pipeline.[38] This positioning ensures that the processor can immediately intercept incoming data and apply backpressure to the receivers, preventing memory accumulation.[38]The memory limiter utilizes soft and hard thresholds [38]:\text{Soft Limit} = \text{limit_mib} - \text{spike_limit_mib}When memory utilization exceeds the soft limit, the collector enters "memory limited mode" and begins rejecting incoming data, returning retriable errors to the upstream client.[38] If utilization exceeds the hard limit (limit_mib), the collector forces synchronous Garbage Collection (GC) execution to reclaim memory.[38]To optimize memory management on Linux container environments (such as Kubernetes pods utilizing cgroups), architects must configure the cgroup memory limits, the collector's processor limits, and the runtime garbage collector environment variables in alignment [38]:limit_percentage: Supported on Linux cgroups, this automatically calculates the hard limit based on the container's physical memory boundaries (e.g., setting 75% on a 1GiB container sets a 750MiB hard limit).[38]GOMEMLIMIT: This Golang runtime variable must be set to exactly 80% of the collector's hard limit (e.g., 0.80 \times 750\text{MiB} = 600\text{MiB}).[38] This forces Golang's GC to run aggressively before the collector hits its soft limit, preventing unexpected OOM container crashes.[38]check_interval: The time between physical memory measurements.[38] For highly spiky workloads, this interval should be decreased to 1s to ensure rapid detection of memory surges, or the spike_limit_mib must be expanded.[38]At the agent level, runtimes like the Java agent optimize memory allocations by leveraging internal object pools instead of continuously allocating short-lived telemetry objects on the heap, mitigating JVM garbage collection pauses.[34] Furthermore, built-in circuit breakers continuously poll stress monitors (such as host CPU and JVM thread states).[34] If the host enters a stressed state, the agent automatically disables telemetry recording, dropping its resource consumption to a bare minimum until system stress is relieved.[34]Interaction with Network Performance Monitoring (NPM)To monitor and manage the overhead introduced by telemetry, organizations must track key network performance indicators, such as bandwidth utilization, throughput, latency, packet loss, jitter, and errors.[39]Telemetry traffic should be evaluated using both active and passive monitoring techniques [40]:Passive Network Telemetry: Observes existing traffic and device events without injecting additional packets.[40] This includes using flow export protocols (like NetFlow or sFlow) and streaming telemetry via gRPC Network Management Interfaces (gNMI).[40] gNMI enables highly efficient streaming telemetry with programmatic management, significantly reducing polling overhead.[40]Active Network Telemetry: Inject synthetic traffic to measure round-trip times and verify path performance.[39, 40]Using gNMI and compressed OTLP streaming helps organizations monitor and control telemetry volume, preventing telemetry traffic from contributing to packet loss, queue delays, or microbursts on production paths.[40]End-to-End Visibility, Dependency Mapping, and Supply Chain ParallelismApplication Dependency Mapping versus Service MappingAchieving end-to-end visibility across a distributed enterprise requires transitioning from single-service analysis to holistic dependency mapping.[3, 41] Application Dependency Mapping (ADM) is the practice of identifying, visualizing, and tracing how individual software components, physical or virtual servers, databases, third-party APIs, and networking layers interact.[3, 41]Architects must distinguish between two complementary mapping layers [41]:Dependency Mapping: Visualizes low-level physical and technical connections between assets, identifying which servers communicate with which databases or network switches.[41]Service Mapping: Aggregates these technical dependencies into logical business services.[41] For instance, it group Web Server A, Payment API B, and Database C under a unified "Customer Checkout Service" map.[41] This business-aligned grouping allows operations teams to immediately assess the financial or customer impact of an underlying infrastructure failure.[41]Ephemerality Gaps and Automated DiscoveryTraditional, manual documentation or static configuration maps are completely inadequate for modern cloud-native environments.[3, 41] Containers launch, scale, and terminate within minutes, serverless functions scale to zero, and microservices are deployed continuously throughout the day.[3] This rapid ephemerality creates severe visibility gaps, as manual records degrade almost instantly.[3, 41]To bridge these gaps, organizations must deploy Automated Service Discovery (ASD) tools that combine two primary mapping approaches [41]:Agentless Discovery: Scans subnets, cloud APIs, and network routing configurations to discover host assets and establish a broad infrastructure baseline.[41]Agent-Based Discovery: Operates inside runtimes and container contexts to intercept actual network flow data and execution graphs, exposing hidden or undocumented dependencies (such as a legacy microservice directly querying an external database bypass-api).[3, 41]These automated mapping layers must undergo continuous reconciliation.[41] Maps must be validated by running monthly scans, cross-referencing maps against real network traffic, and validating paths during scheduled change windows.[41] Without this continuous validation, maps become untrustworthy, extending incident resolution times and increasing the risk of cascading failures during routine updates.[41]Operational Mapping Parallel: IT versus Supply Chain VisibilityThe structural paradigms of IT dependency mapping are mirrored in physical supply chain and logistics networks.[42, 43, 44] In logistics, end-to-end visibility represents the ability to track the movement, status, and physical condition of cargo across disparate carriers, ports, warehouses, and final delivery locations.[44] Just as software applications suffer from siloed telemetry (metrics in one database, logs in another), logistics networks face fragmented visibility due to disparate carrier APIs, legacy EDI messaging, and manual check-ins.[3, 42, 44]Both environments solve these fragmentation challenges using identical architectural principles:Unified Operational Data Layer: Logistics networks utilize data lakes to reconcile physical asset identities (tractors, shipping containers, liveness sensors) with immutable, automated timestamps, eliminating duplicate records and "unknown dwell" errors.[42, 44] This directly corresponds to the OpenTelemetry Collector aggregating metrics, logs, and trace spans into a single source of truth.[1, 44]Real-Time Automated Event Capture: Relying on human manual input (whether an engineer logging an incident or a dock worker manually scanning a cargo manifest) introduces data latency and low trust.[42, 43] Both systems require automated, real-time event capture—such as automatic traceparent propagation across APIs or automated liveness lacing at shipping ports—to transition from reactive firefighting to proactive risk management.[22, 42, 43]Recommendations and Core Architectural GuidelinesEstablishing a resilient and cost-effective enterprise observability platform requires a unified, standard-based approach to configuration, correlation, and sampling.First, standardizing on the OpenTelemetry API is essential to mitigate vendor lock-in. Decoupling the application code from the exporter configuration allows organizations to change downstream backends via configuration updates rather than complex codebase refactoring. Runtimes should utilize the BatchSpanProcessor to buffer telemetry asynchronously, protecting high-throughput application paths from thread blocking and network latency.Second, organizations should enforce strict naming and namespace uniformity by implementing OpenTelemetry Semantic Conventions. Custom attributes must be lowercase and dot-separated, restricted to clear organizational namespaces (such as company.* or app.*) to prevent collisions with protected standard namespaces. High-cardinality keys must be excluded from metrics and isolated to traces and logs to prevent metrics explosion and memory exhaustion in time-series databases. Furthermore, sensitive headers, query parameters, and database statements must be systematically redacted at the collector tier using the attributes processor.Third, bidirectional trace-log correlation should be implemented by injecting trace IDs and span IDs directly into structured JSON logs. Runtimes must utilize compatible, structured logging libraries and Mapped Diagnostic Context (MDC) configurations to ensure standard format consistency. Cross-process context propagation must default to W3C Trace Context standards, and asynchronous message boundaries (such as Kafka, RabbitMQ, or SQS) must write trace context directly into message attributes to prevent trace tree fragmentation.Fourth, organizations should deploy a layered hybrid sampling strategy to manage costs without sacrificing critical visibility. Probabilistic head-based sampling should be applied at the application tier to control baseline network egress, while tail-based sampling rules at the collector tier filter and retain 100% of errors, latency anomalies, and high-value transactional paths.Finally, collector stability must be protected by placing the memory_limiter processor first in every pipeline. Container memory limits must align with the cgroup configuration, and runtime environment variables (such as GOMEMLIMIT) must be set to exactly 80% of the collector's hard limit to prevent sudden OOM crashes. This pipeline optimization, combined with automated, reconciled application dependency mapping, ensures that organizations maintain deep, real-time visibility across complex distributed architectures.OpenTelemetry Observability: An In-Depth Look at Features and Best Practices -
